      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_bodies_and_areas_of_conflict">Regional bodies and areas of conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Regional bodies and areas of conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In addition to the states, there also exist regional administrative bodies established by peace agreements between the central government and rebel groups. </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Darfur_Regional_Authority" title="Darfur Regional Authority">Darfur Regional Authority</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Darfur_Peace_Agreement" title="Darfur Peace Agreement">Darfur Peace Agreement</a> to act as a co-ordinating body for the states that make up the region of <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a>.</li> 
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Sudan_States_Coordinating_Council" title="Eastern Sudan States Coordinating Council">Eastern Sudan States Coordinating Council</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Sudan_Peace_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Sudan Peace Agreement">Eastern Sudan Peace Agreement</a> between the Sudanese Government and the rebel <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(Sudan)" title="Eastern Front (Sudan)">Eastern Front</a> to act as a coordinating body for the three eastern states.</li> 
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Abyei" title="Abyei">Abyei Area</a>, located on the border between South Sudan and the Republic of the Sudan, currently has a special administrative status and is governed by an <a href="/wiki/Abyei_Area_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Abyei Area Administration">Abyei Area Administration</a>. It was due to hold a <a href="/wiki/Abyei_status_referendum,_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="Abyei status referendum, 2011">referendum</a> in 2011 on whether to join an independent <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> or remain part of the Republic of the Sudan.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Sudan" title="Economy of Sudan">Economy of Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Sudan" title="Telecommunications in Sudan">Telecommunications in Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Sudan" title="Transport in Sudan">Transport in Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png/180px-Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png/270px-Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png/360px-Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png 2x" data-file-width="510" data-file-height="762"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_Map_Oelgas.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">gas</a> concessions in Sudan – 2004
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2010, Sudan was considered the 17th-fastest-growing economy<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> in the world and the rapid development of the country largely from oil profits even when facing international sanctions was noted by <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> in a 2006 article.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Because of the secession of <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, which contained over 80 percent of Sudan's oilfields, Sudan entered a phase of <a href="/wiki/Stagflation" title="Stagflation">stagflation</a>, GDP growth slowed to 3.4 percent in 2014, 3.1 percent in 2015 and is projected to recover slowly to 3.7 percent in 2016 while inflation remained as high as 21.8% as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Sudan's GDP fell from US$123.053 billion in 2017 to US$40.852 billion in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Even with the oil profits before the secession of South Sudan, Sudan still faced formidable economic problems, and its growth was still a rise from a very low level of per capita output. The economy of Sudan has been steadily growing over the 2000s, and according to a World Bank report the overall growth in GDP in 2010 was 5.2 percent compared to 2009 growth of 4.2 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-23">[23]</a></sup> This growth was sustained even during the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Darfur" title="War in Darfur">war in Darfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Sudan_Autonomous_Region_(2005%E2%80%9311)" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Sudan Autonomous Region (2005–11)">period of southern autonomy</a> preceding South Sudan's independence.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Oil</a> was Sudan's main export, with production increasing dramatically during the late 2000s, in the years before South Sudan gained independence in July 2011. With rising oil revenues, the Sudanese economy was booming, with a growth rate of about nine percent in 2007. The independence of oil-rich <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, however, placed most major <a href="/wiki/Oilfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Oilfield">oilfields</a> out of the Sudanese government's direct control and oil production in Sudan fell from around 450,000 barrels per day (72,000&nbsp;m<sup>3</sup>/d) to under 60,000 barrels per day (9,500&nbsp;m<sup>3</sup>/d). Production has since recovered to hover around 250,000 barrels per day (40,000&nbsp;m<sup>3</sup>/d) for 2014–15.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In order to export oil, South Sudan relies on a pipeline to <a href="/wiki/Port_Sudan" title="Port Sudan">Port Sudan</a> on Sudan's <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast, as South Sudan is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a>, as well as the oil refining facilities in Sudan. In August 2012, Sudan and South Sudan agreed a deal to transport South Sudanese oil through Sudanese pipelines to Port Sudan.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> is one of Sudan's major trading partners, China owns a 40 percent share in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Nile_Petroleum_Operating_Company" title="Greater Nile Petroleum Operating Company">Greater Nile Petroleum Operating Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> The country also sells Sudan small arms, which have been used in military operations such as the conflicts in Darfur and <a href="/wiki/South_Kordofan_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="South Kordofan conflict">South Kordofan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>While historically agriculture remains the main source of income and employment hiring of over 80 percent of Sudanese, and makes up a third of the economic sector, oil production drove most of Sudan's post-2000 growth. Currently, the International Monetary Fund (IMF) is working hand in hand with Khartoum government to implement sound macroeconomic policies. This follows a turbulent period in the 1980s when debt-ridden Sudan's relations with the IMF and World Bank soured, culminating in its eventual suspension from the IMF.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1992[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_June_2013]]<sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;>&amp;#91;<i>[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|<span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(June_2013)&quot;>page&amp;nbsp;needed</span>]]</i>&amp;#93;</sup>_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1992[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_June_2013]]<sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;>&amp;#91;<i>[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|<span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(June_2013)&quot;>page&amp;nbsp;needed</span>]]</i>&amp;#93;</sup>-228">[228]</a></sup> The program has been in place since the early 1990s, and also work-out exchange rate and reserve of foreign exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-23">[23]</a></sup> Since 1997, Sudan has been implementing the <a href="/wiki/Macroeconomics" title="Macroeconomics">macroeconomic</a> reforms recommended by the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Agricultural production remains Sudan's most-important sector, employing 80 percent of the workforce and contributing 39 percent of GDP, but most farms remain rain-fed and susceptible to drought. Instability, adverse weather and weak world-agricultural prices ensures that much of the population will remain at or below the poverty line for years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Merowe_Dam" title="Merowe Dam">Merowe Dam</a>, also known as Merowe Multi-Purpose Hydro Project or Hamdab Dam, is a large construction project in northern Sudan, about 350 kilometres (220&nbsp;mi) north of the capital, Khartoum. It is situated on the River Nile, close to the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_cataract_of_the_Nile" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourth cataract of the Nile">Fourth Cataract</a> where the river divides into multiple smaller branches with large islands in between. <a href="/wiki/Merowe,_Sudan" title="Merowe, Sudan">Merowe</a> is a city about 40 kilometres (25&nbsp;mi) downstream from the dam's construction site. </p>
      <p>The main purpose of the dam will be the generation of electricity. Its dimensions make it the largest contemporary hydropower project in Africa. The construction of the dam was finished December 2008, supplying more than 90 percent of the population with electricity. Other gas-powered generating stations are operational in Khartoum State and other states. </p>
      <p>According to the Corruptions Perception Index, Sudan is one of the most corrupt nations in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Global_Hunger_Index" title="Global Hunger Index">Global Hunger Index</a> of 2013, Sudan has an GHI indicator value of 27.0 indicating that the nation has an 'Alarming Hunger Situation.' It is rated the fifth hungriest nation in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> According to the 2015 <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (HDI) Sudan ranked the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">167th</a> place in human development, indicating Sudan still has one of the lowest human development rates in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> In 2014, 45% of the population lives on less than US$3.20 per day, up from 43% in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Sudan" title="Demographics of Sudan">Demographics of Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg/180px-Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg/270px-Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg/360px-Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg 2x" data-file-width="734" data-file-height="1185"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_-_smiling_lady.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Student from <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 10px"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th colspan="4" style="text-align:center; background:#cfb;">Population in Sudan<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-233">[233]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-234">[234]</a></sup> </th>

         <td style="text-align:right;">27.2 </td>

         <td style="text-align:right;">41.8 </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>In Sudan's 2008 census, the population of northern, western and eastern Sudan was recorded to be over 30 million.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> This puts present estimates of the population of Sudan after the secession of <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> at a little over 30 million people. This is a significant increase over the past two decades, as the 1983 census put the total population of Sudan, including present-day South Sudan, at 21.6 million.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> The population of Greater Khartoum (including <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omdurman" title="Omdurman">Omdurman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Khartoum_North" title="Khartoum North">Khartoum North</a>) is growing rapidly and was recorded to be 5.2 million. </p>
      <p>Aside from being a refugee-generating country, Sudan also hosts a large population of refugees from other countries. According to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">UNHCR</a> statistics, more than 1.1 million refugees and asylum seekers lived in Sudan in August, 2019. The majority of this population came from <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> (858,607 people), <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> (123,413), <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> (93,502), <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> (14,201), the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a> (11,713) and <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> (3,100). Apart from these, the UNHCR report 1,864,195 <a href="/wiki/Internally_displaced_person" title="Internally displaced person">Internally Displaced Persons</a> (IDP's).<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> Sudan is a party to the 1951 <a href="/wiki/Convention_Relating_to_the_Status_of_Refugees" title="Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees">Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Eisa_shikawi.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Eisa_shikawi.JPG/220px-Eisa_shikawi.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Eisa_shikawi.JPG/330px-Eisa_shikawi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Eisa_shikawi.JPG/440px-Eisa_shikawi.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Eisa_shikawi.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Sudanese Arab">Sudanese Arab</a> of <a href="/wiki/Manasir" title="Manasir">Al-Manasir</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabs" title="Sudanese Arabs">Arab</a> presence is estimated at 70% of the population. Others include North Sudan <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">Nubians</a>, Zurga (South and West Sudan), and <a href="/wiki/Copts_in_Sudan" title="Copts in Sudan">Copts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr1_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr1-238">[238]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Sudan has 597 groups that speak over 400 different languages and dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabs" title="Sudanese Arabs">Sudanese Arabs</a> are by far the largest ethnic group in Sudan. They are almost entirely Muslims; while the majority speak <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabic" title="Sudanese Arabic">Sudanese Arabic</a>, some other Arab tribes speak different Arabic dialects like <a href="/wiki/Awadia_and_Fadnia_tribes" title="Awadia and Fadnia tribes">Awadia and Fadnia tribes</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Arakieen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Arakieen (page does not exist)">Bani Arak</a> tribes who speak <a href="/wiki/Najdi_Arabic" title="Najdi Arabic">Najdi Arabic</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Beni_%E1%B8%A4ass%C4%81n" title="Beni Ḥassān">Beni Ḥassān</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ashraf">Al-Ashraf</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kawhla&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kawhla (page does not exist)">Kawhla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rashaida" class="mw-redirect" title="Rashaida">Rashaida</a> who speak <a href="/wiki/Hejazi_Arabic" title="Hejazi Arabic">Hejazi Arabic</a>. In addition, the Western province comprises various ethnic groups, while a few Arab <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> of the northern <a href="/wiki/Rizeigat" class="mw-redirect" title="Rizeigat">Rizeigat</a> and others who speak Sudanese Arabic share the same culture and backgrounds of the Sudanese Arabs. </p>
      <p>The majority of Arabised and indigenous tribes like the <a href="/wiki/Fur_people" title="Fur people">Fur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_people" title="Zaghawa people">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Borgo_people&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Borgo people (page does not exist)">Borgo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Masalit_people" title="Masalit people">Masalit</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Baggara" title="Baggara">Baggara</a> ethnic groups, who speak <a href="/wiki/Chadian_Arabic" title="Chadian Arabic">Chadian Arabic</a>, show less cultural integration because of cultural, linguistic and genealogical variations with other Arab and Arabised tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuliman2010115_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuliman2010115-241">[241]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Sudanese Arabs of Northern and Eastern parts descend primarily from migrants from the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> and intermarriages with the pre-existing indigenous populations of Sudan, especially the <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubian people</a>, who also share a common history with <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Additionally, a few pre-Islamic Arabian tribes existed in Sudan from earlier migrations into the region from Western Arabia, although most Arabs in Sudan are dated from migrations after the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The vast majority of Arab tribes in Sudan migrated into the Sudan in the 12th century, intermarried with the indigenous Nubian and other African populations and introduced Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-google16_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google16-243">[243]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Sudan consists of numerous other non-Arabic groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Masalit_people" title="Masalit people">Masalit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_people" title="Zaghawa people">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fulani" class="mw-redirect" title="Fulani">Fulani</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_Nubians&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Northern Nubians (page does not exist)">Northern Nubians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuba" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuba">Nuba</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Beja_people" title="Beja people">Beja people</a>. </p>
      <p>There is also a small, but prominent <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Sudan" title="Greeks in Sudan">Greek community</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Sudan" title="Languages of Sudan">Languages of Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Rashaida_family.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Rashaida_family.png/180px-Rashaida_family.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Rashaida_family.png/270px-Rashaida_family.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Rashaida_family.png/360px-Rashaida_family.png 2x" data-file-width="1419" data-file-height="1671"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Rashaida_family.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Arabic-speaking <a href="/wiki/Rashaida_people" title="Rashaida people">Rashaida</a> came to Sudan from <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> about 175 years ago.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Approximately 70 languages are native to Sudan.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-244">[244]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabic" title="Sudanese Arabic">Sudanese Arabic</a> is the most widely spoken language in the country. It is the <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">variety of Arabic</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> language of the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> branch spoken throughout Sudan. The dialect has borrowed much vocabulary from local Nilo-Saharan languages (<a href="/wiki/Nobiin_language" title="Nobiin language">Nobiin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fur_language" title="Fur language">Fur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_language" title="Zaghawa language">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maba_language" title="Maba language">Mabang</a>). This has resulted in a variety of Arabic that is unique to Sudan, reflecting the way in which the country has been influenced by Nilotic, Arab, and western cultures. Few nomads in Sudan still have similar accents to the ones in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. Other important languages include <a href="/wiki/Beja_language" title="Beja language">Beja</a> (Bedawi) along the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, with perhaps two million speakers. It is the language from the Afroasiatic family's <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cushitic language">Cushitic</a> branch that is today spoken in the territory. The second most spoken language in eastern Sudan is the <a href="/wiki/Tigre_language" title="Tigre language">Tigre language</a>, spoken by the other portion of the <a href="/wiki/Beja_people" title="Beja people">Beja</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bani-amir&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bani-amir (page does not exist)">Bani-amir</a> and by the <a href="/wiki/Tigre_people" title="Tigre people">Tigre people</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>As with South Sudan, a number of <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan languages</a> are also spoken in Sudan. <a href="/wiki/Fur_language" title="Fur language">Fur</a> speakers inhabit the west (<a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a>), with perhaps a million speakers. There are likewise various <a href="/wiki/Nubian_languages" title="Nubian languages">Nubian languages</a> along the Nile in the north. The most linguistically diverse region in the country is the <a href="/wiki/Nuba_Hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuba Hills">Nuba Hills</a> area in Kordofan, inhabited by speakers of multiple language families, with Darfur and other border regions being second. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger–Congo</a> family is represented by many of the <a href="/wiki/Kordofanian_languages" title="Kordofanian languages">Kordofanian languages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> by <a href="/wiki/Domari_language" title="Domari language">Domari</a> (Gypsy) and English. Historically, <a href="/wiki/Old_Nubian" title="Old Nubian">Old Nubian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> were the languages of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Nubia" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Nubia">Christian Nubia</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Meroitic_language" title="Meroitic language">Meroitic</a> was the language of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a>, which conquered Egypt. </p>
      <p>Sudan also has multiple regional sign languages, which are not <a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a>. A 2009 proposal for a unified <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Sign_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sudanese Sign Language">Sudanese Sign Language</a> had been worked out, but was not widely known.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Prior to 2005, Arabic was the nation's sole <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LeClerc_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeClerc-246">[246]</a></sup> In the 2005 constitution, Sudan's official languages became Arabic and English.<sup id="cite_ref-Const_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Const-247">[247]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_areas">Urban areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Urban areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

       Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Sudan" title="List of cities in Sudan">List of cities in Sudan</a>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Sudan" title="Religion in Sudan">Religion in Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Masjid_Al-Nilin,_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg/300px-Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg/450px-Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg/600px-Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="525"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Masjid_Al-Nilin,_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Masjid Al-Nilin, August 2007
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;"> 
       <div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;"> 
        <table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;"> 
         <tbody>
          <tr style="background:#ddd">
           <th style="text-align:center;" colspan="5">Religion in Sudan<sup id="cite_ref-CWF_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CWF-249">[249]</a></sup></th>
          </tr> 
          <tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;"> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding:0 4px; text-align:left;">religion</td> 
           <td style="width:100px; text-align:left;"></td> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding:0 4px; width:1em; text-align:right;">percent</td> 
          </tr> 
          <tr> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">Islam</td> 
           <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
            <div style="background:Green; width:97%; overflow: hidden;">
              
            </div></td> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">97%</td> 
          </tr> 
          <tr> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">African traditional religion</td> 
           <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
            <div style="background:Brown; width:1.5%; overflow: hidden;">
              
            </div></td> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">1.5%</td> 
          </tr> 
          <tr> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">Christianity</td> 
           <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
            <div style="background:Blue; width:1.5%; overflow: hidden;">
              
            </div></td> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">1.5%</td> 
          </tr> 
         </tbody>
        </table> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
      <p>At the 2011 division which split off South Sudan, over 97% of the population in the remaining Sudan adheres to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Islam_in_Sudan1_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_in_Sudan1-250">[250]</a></sup> Most Muslims are divided between two groups: <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> and Salafi (Ansar Al Sunnah) Muslims. Two popular divisions of Sufism, the Ansar and the Khatmia, are associated with the opposition Umma and Democratic Unionist parties, respectively. Only the Darfur region has traditionally been bereft of the Sufi brotherhoods common in the rest of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Long-established groups of <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a> Christians exist in Khartoum and other northern cities. <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eritrean_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Eritrean Orthodox</a> communities also exist in Khartoum and eastern Sudan, largely made up of refugees and migrants from the past few decades. The <a href="/wiki/Sudan_Evangelical_Presbyterian_Church" title="Sudan Evangelical Presbyterian Church">Sudan Evangelical Presbyterian Church</a> also has membership.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">along with which others within current borders?</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Religious identity plays a role in the country's political divisions. Northern and western Muslims have dominated the country's political and economic system since independence. The NCP draws much of its support from <a href="/wiki/Islamists" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamists">Islamists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salafis" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafis">Salafis</a>/<a href="/wiki/Wahhabis" class="mw-redirect" title="Wahhabis">Wahhabis</a> and other conservative Arab Muslims in the north. The <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Umma</a> Party has traditionally attracted Arab followers of the Ansar sect of Sufism as well as non-Arab Muslims from Darfur and Kordofan. The Democratic Unionist Party (DUP) includes both Arab and non-Arab Muslims in the north and east, especially those in the Khatmia Sufi sect. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sudan" title="Music of Sudan">Music of Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Sudan" title="Cinema of Sudan">Cinema of Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photography_of_Sudan" title="Photography of Sudan">Photography of Sudan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sudanese_writers" title="List of Sudanese writers">List of Sudanese writers</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Sudanese culture melds the behaviors, practices, and beliefs of about 578 ethnic groups, communicating in 145 different languages, in a region microcosmic of Africa, with geographic extremes varying from sandy desert to tropical forest. Recent evidence suggests that while most citizens of the country identify strongly with both Sudan and their religion, Arab and African supranational identities are much more polarising and contested.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sudan" title="Music of Sudan">Music of Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_(8625532075).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg/220px-Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg/330px-Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg/440px-Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_(8625532075).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Dervish" title="Dervish">dervish</a> drums up the Friday afternoon crowd in <a href="/wiki/Omdurman" title="Omdurman">Omdurman</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Sudan has a rich and unique musical culture that has been through chronic instability and repression during the modern history of Sudan. Beginning with the imposition of strict <a href="/wiki/Salafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafi">Salafi</a> interpretation of <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i> law in 1989, many of the country's most prominent poets, like <a href="/wiki/Mahjoub_Sharif" title="Mahjoub Sharif">Mahjoub Sharif</a>, were imprisoned while others, like <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mohammed_el_Amin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mohammed el Amin (page does not exist)">Mohammed el Amin</a> (returned to Sudan in the mid-1990s) and <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Wardi" title="Mohammed Wardi">Mohammed Wardi</a> (returned to Sudan 2003), fled to Cairo. Traditional music suffered too, with traditional <a href="/wiki/Z%C4%81r" title="Zār">Zār</a> ceremonies being interrupted and drums confiscated <sup class="reference" id="ref_roughguidea"><a href="#endnote_roughguidea">[1]</a></sup>. At the same time European militaries contributed to the development of Sudanese music by introducing new instruments and styles; military bands, especially the Scottish <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipes</a>, were renowned, and set traditional music to <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">military march</a> music. The march <i>March Shulkawi No 1</i>, is an example, set to the sounds of the <a href="/wiki/Shilluk_people" title="Shilluk people">Shilluk</a>. Northern Sudan listens to different music than the rest of Sudan. A type of music called Aldlayib uses a musical instrument called the Tambur. The Tambur has five strings and is made from wood and makes music accompanied by the voices of human applause and singing artists. This music has a perfect blend that gives the area of the Northern State a special character. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema_and_photography">Cinema and photography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Cinema and photography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Sudan" title="Cinema of Sudan">Cinema of Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photography_of_Sudan" title="Photography of Sudan">Photography of Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Sudan" title="Cinema of Sudan">cinema of Sudan</a> began with <a href="/wiki/Cinematography" title="Cinematography">cinematography</a> by the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="History of Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">British colonial presence</a> in the early 20th century. After independence in 1956, a vigorous documentary film tradition was established, but financial pressures and serious constraints imposed by the <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> government led to the decline of filmmaking from the 1990s onwards. Since the 2010s, several initiatives have shown an encouraging revival of filmmaking and public interest in film shows and festivals, albeit limited mainly to Khartoum. </p>
      <p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Photography_of_Sudan" title="Photography of Sudan">photography in Sudan</a> goes back to the 1880s and the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Sudan" title="Turkish Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian rule</a>. As in other countries, the growing importance of photography for <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass media</a> like newspapers, as well as for amateur photographers led to a wider photographic <a href="/wiki/Documentation" title="Documentation">documentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photojournalism#Golden_age" title="Photojournalism">use of photographs</a> in Sudan during the 20th century and beyond. In the 21st century, photography in Sudan has undergone important changes, mainly due to <a href="/wiki/Digital_photography" title="Digital photography">digital photography</a> and distribution through <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">internet</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The most popular sports in Sudan are athletics (<a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">track and field</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Though not as successful as football, <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Team_handball" class="mw-redirect" title="Team handball">handball</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a> are also popular in Sudan. In the 1960s and 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_national_basketball_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Sudan national basketball team">national basketball team</a> finished among the continent's top teams.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Nowadays, it is only a minor force. </p>
      <p>Sudanese football has a long history. Sudan was one of the four African nations – the others being Egypt, Ethiopia and South Africa – which formed African football. Sudan hosted the first <a href="/wiki/African_Cup_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="African Cup of Nations">African Cup of Nations</a> in 1956, and has won the African Cup of Nations once, in 1970. Two years later, the Sudan's National Football Team participated in the <a href="/wiki/1972_Summer_Olympics" title="1972 Summer Olympics">1972 Olympic Games</a> in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">nation's capital</a> is home to the Khartoum League, which is considered to be the oldest football league in Africa.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Sudanese football teams such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Hilal_Club_(Omdurman)" title="Al-Hilal Club (Omdurman)">Al-Hilal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Merrikh_SC" title="Al-Merrikh SC">Al-Merrikh</a>, and Abdelgadir Osman FC are among the nation's strongest teams. Other teams like Khartoum, El-Neel, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Nidal_El-Nahud&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Al-Nidal El-Nahud (page does not exist)">Al-Nidal El-Nahud</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hay-Al_Arab&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hay-Al Arab (page does not exist)">Hay-Al Arab</a>, are also starting to grow in popularity. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing">Clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bedscha.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Bedscha.jpg/220px-Bedscha.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Bedscha.jpg/330px-Bedscha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Bedscha.jpg/440px-Bedscha.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="535"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bedscha.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bejia men wearing galabiyas
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Most Sudanese wear either traditional or western attire. A traditional garb widely worn by Sudanese men is the <a href="/wiki/Jalabiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalabiya">galabiya</a>, which is a loose-fitting, long-sleeved, collarless ankle-length garment also common to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. The galabiya is often accompanied by a large turban and a scarf, and the garment may be white, colored, striped, and made of fabric varying in thickness, depending on the season of the year and personal preferences. </p>
      <p>The most common dress for Sudanese women is the <i>thobe</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Thawb" title="Thawb">thawb</a></i>, pronounced <i>tobe</i> in Sudanese dialect. The thobe is a white or colorful long, one piece cloth that women wrap around their inner garments, usually covering their head and hair. </p>
      <p>Due to a 1991 penal code (<i>Public Order Law</i>), women were not allowed to wear trousers in public, because it was interpreted as an "obscene outfit." The punishment for wearing trousers could be up to 40 lashes, but after being found guilty in 2009, one woman was fined the equivalent of 200 U.S. dollars instead.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Sudan" title="Education in Sudan">Education in Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Faculty_of_Science_(University_of_Khartoum)_002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg/220px-Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg/330px-Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg/440px-Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Faculty_of_Science_(University_of_Khartoum)_002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Khartoum_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Khartoum University">Khartoum University</a> established in 1902
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Education in Sudan is free and compulsory for children aged 6 to 13 years, although more than 40% of children do not go to schools due to the economic situation. Environmental and social factors also increase the difficulty of getting to school, especially for girls.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> Primary education consists of eight years, followed by three years of secondary education. The former educational ladder 6 + 3 + 3 was changed in 1990. The primary language at all levels is Arabic. Schools are concentrated in urban areas; many in the west have been damaged or destroyed by years of civil war. In 2001 the World Bank estimated that primary enrollment was 46 percent of eligible pupils and 21 percent of secondary students. Enrollment varies widely, falling below 20 percent in some provinces. The literacy rate is 70.2% of total population, male: 79.6%, female: 60.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_research">Science and research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Science and research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Sudan has around 25–30 universities; instruction is primarily in Arabic or English. Education at the secondary and university levels has been seriously hampered by the requirement that most males perform military service before completing their education.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-255">[255]</a></sup> In addition, the "Islamisation" encouraged by president Al-Bashir alienated many researchers: the official language of instruction in universities was changed from English to Arabic and Islamic courses became mandatory. Internal science funding withered.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-256">[256]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>, more than 3,000 Sudanese researchers left the country between 2002 and 2014. By 2013, the country had a mere 19 researchers for every 100,000 citizens, or 1/30 the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, according to the Sudanese National Centre for Research. In 2015, Sudan published only about 500 scientific papers.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-256">[256]</a></sup> In comparison, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, a country of similar population size, publishes on the order of 10,000 papers per year.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Sudan" title="Health in Sudan">Health in Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Sudan has a <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> of 65.1 years according to the latest data for the year 2019 from macrotrends.net<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> Infant mortality in 2016 was 44.8 per 1,000.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> estimates that 87% of Sudanese females between the ages of 15 to 49 have had <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> performed on them.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> </p>
